<p>By late 2006, Google established a new headquarters for its AdWords division in <a href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan" title="Ann Arbor, Michigan">Ann Arbor, Michigan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> In November 2006, Google opened offices on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon" title="Carnegie Mellon">Carnegie Mellon</a>'s campus in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, focusing on shopping-related advertisement coding and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smartphone_applications" title="Smartphone applications">smartphone applications</a> and programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup> Other office locations in the U.S. include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>; <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a>; <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" title="Seattle, Washington">Seattle, Washington</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kirkland,_Washington" title="Kirkland, Washington">Kirkland, Washington</a>; <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Michigan" title="Birmingham, Michigan">Birmingham, Michigan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Reston,_Virginia" title="Reston, Virginia">Reston, Virginia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In October 2006, the company announced plans to install thousands of <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> to provide up to 1.6 mega<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> of electricity, enough to satisfy approximately 30% of the campus' energy needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-solar-326">[326]</a></sup> The system will be the largest <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">solar power system</a> constructed on a U.S. corporate campus and one of the largest on any corporate site in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_326-1"><a href="#cite_note-solar-326">[326]</a></sup> In addition, Google announced in 2009 that it was deploying herds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goats" title="Goats">goats</a> to keep grassland around the Googleplex short, helping to prevent the threat from seasonal bush fires while also reducing the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> of mowing the extensive grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup> The idea of trimming lawns using goats originated from <a href="/wiki/Bob_Widlar" title="Bob Widlar">Bob Widlar</a>, an engineer who worked for <a href="/wiki/National_Semiconductor" title="National Semiconductor">National Semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup> In 2008, Google faced accusations in <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper's Magazine">Harper's Magazine</a></i> of being an "energy glutton". The company was accused of employing its "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_be_evil" title="Don't be evil">Don't be evil</a>" motto and its public <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy-saving" title="Energy-saving">energy-saving</a> campaigns to cover up or make up for the massive amounts of energy its servers require.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Internationally, Google has over 78 offices in more than 50 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup> It also has product research and development operations in cities around the world, namely <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a> (birthplace location of <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">Google Maps</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> (part of <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> development).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In November 2013, Google announced plans for a new <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> headquarter, a notable 1 million square foot office able to accommodate 4,500 employees. Recognized as one of the biggest ever commercial property acquisitions at the time of the deal's announcement in January,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup> Google submitted plans for the new headquarter to the <a href="/wiki/Camden_London_Borough_Council" title="Camden London Borough Council">Camden Council</a> in June 2017. The new building, if approved, will feature a rooftop garden with a running track, giant moving blinds, a swimming pool, and a multi-use games area for sports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In May 2015, Google announced its intention to create its own campus in <a href="/wiki/Hyderabad" title="Hyderabad">Hyderabad</a>, India. The new campus, reported to be the company's largest outside the United States, will accommodate 13,000 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Since  1998,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Google has been designing special, temporary alternate logos to place on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homepage" title="Homepage">homepage</a> intended to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Holiday" title="Holiday">holidays</a>, events, achievements and people. The first Google <a href="/wiki/Doodle" title="Doodle">Doodle</a> was in honor of the <a href="/wiki/Burning_Man" title="Burning Man">Burning Man Festival</a> of 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup> The doodle was designed by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Brin" title="Sergey Brin">Sergey Brin</a> to notify users of their absence in case the servers crashed. Subsequent Google Doodles were designed by an outside contractor, until Larry and Sergey asked then-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intern" title="Intern">intern</a> <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Hwang" title="Dennis Hwang">Dennis Hwang</a> to design a logo for <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day" title="Bastille Day">Bastille Day</a> in 2000. From that point onward, Doodles have been organized and created by a team of employees termed "Doodlers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google has a tradition of creating <a href="/wiki/April_Fools%27_Day" title="April Fools' Day">April Fools' Day</a> jokes. On April 1, 2000, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google%27s_hoaxes#2000" title="Google's hoaxes">Google MentalPlex</a> allegedly featured the use of mental power to search the web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mentalplex_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-mentalplex-342">[342]</a></sup> In 2007, Google announced a free Internet service called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TiSP" title="TiSP">TiSP</a>, or Toilet Internet Service Provider, where one obtained a connection by flushing one end of a <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fiber-optic</a> cable down their toilet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TiSP_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-TiSP-343">[343]</a></sup> Also in 2007, Google's Gmail page displayed an announcement for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gmail_Paper" title="Gmail Paper">Gmail Paper</a>, allowing users to have email messages printed and shipped to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmail_paper_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-gmail_paper-344">[344]</a></sup>
In 2008, Google announced Gmail Custom time where users could change the time that the email was sent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2010, Google changed its company name to Topeka in honor of <a href="/wiki/Topeka,_Kansas" title="Topeka, Kansas">Topeka, Kansas</a>, whose mayor changed the city's name to Google for a short amount of time in an attempt to sway Google's decision in its new <a href="/wiki/Google_Fiber" title="Google Fiber">Google Fiber Project</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Blog:_A_Different_Kind_of_Company_Name_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Blog:_A_Different_Kind_of_Company_Name-346">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup> In 2011, Google announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gmail_Motion" title="Gmail Motion">Gmail Motion</a>, an interactive way of controlling Gmail and the computer with body movements via the user's webcam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[348]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's services contain <a href="/wiki/Easter_egg_(media)" title="Easter egg (media)">easter eggs</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Chef" title="Swedish Chef">Swedish Chef</a>'s "Bork bork bork," <a href="/wiki/Pig_Latin" title="Pig Latin">Pig Latin</a>, "Hacker" or <a href="/wiki/Leet" title="Leet">leetspeak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elmer_Fudd" title="Elmer Fudd">Elmer Fudd</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Talk_Like_a_Pirate_Day" title="International Talk Like a Pirate Day">Pirate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Klingon_language" title="Klingon language">Klingon</a> as language selections for its search engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup> The search engine calculator provides the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Answer_to_the_Ultimate_Question_of_Life,_the_Universe,_and_Everything" title="Answer to the Ultimate Question of Life, the Universe, and Everything">Answer to the Ultimate Question of Life, the Universe, and Everything</a> from <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams">Douglas Adams</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hitchhiker%27s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy" title="The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy">The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[350]</a></sup> When searching the word "recursion", the spell-checker's result for the properly spelled word is exactly the same word, creating a recursive link.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[351]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>When searching for the word "<a href="/wiki/Anagram" title="Anagram">anagram</a>," meaning a rearrangement of letters from one word to form other valid words, Google's suggestion feature displays "Did you mean: nag a ram?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[352]</a></sup> In Google Maps, searching for directions between places separated by large bodies of water, such as Los Angeles and Tokyo, results in instructions to "<a href="/wiki/Kayak" title="Kayak">kayak</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>." During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup_2010" title="FIFA World Cup 2010">FIFA World Cup 2010</a>, search queries including "<a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">World Cup</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>" caused the "Goooo...gle" page indicator at the bottom of every result page to read "Goooo...al!" instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2004, Google formed the not-for-profit philanthropic <a href="/wiki/Google.org" title="Google.org">Google.org</a>, with a start-up fund of $1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philanthropy_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-philanthropy-354">[354]</a></sup> The mission of the organization is to create awareness about <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, global public health, and global poverty. One of its first projects was to develop a viable <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> that can attain 100 miles per gallon. Google hired <a href="/wiki/Larry_Brilliant" title="Larry Brilliant">Larry Brilliant</a> as the program's executive director in 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup> and Megan Smith has since<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> replaced him has director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2008, Google announced its "project 10<sup>100</sup>" which accepted ideas for how to help the community and then allowed Google users to vote on their favorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[357]</a></sup> After two years of silence, during which many wondered what had happened to the program,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[358]</a></sup> Google revealed the winners of the project, giving a total of ten million dollars to various ideas ranging from non-profit organizations that promote education to a website that intends to make all legal documents public and online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[359]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In March 2007, in partnership with the <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Sciences_Research_Institute" title="Mathematical Sciences Research Institute">Mathematical Sciences Research Institute</a> (MSRI), Google hosted the first <a href="/wiki/Julia_Robinson_Mathematics_Festival" title="Julia Robinson Mathematics Festival">Julia Robinson Mathematics Festival</a> at its headquarters in Mountain View.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[360]</a></sup> In 2011, Google donated 1 million euros to <a href="/wiki/International_Mathematical_Olympiad" title="International Mathematical Olympiad">International Mathematical Olympiad</a> to support the next five annual International Mathematical Olympiads (2011–2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[361]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[362]</a></sup> In July 2012, Google launched a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legalize_Love" title="Legalize Love">Legalize Love</a>" campaign in support of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_rights" title="Gay rights">gay rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[363]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google uses various <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax avoidance strategies</a>. Out of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_information_technology_companies" title="List of the largest information technology companies">five largest American technology companies</a>, it pays the lowest taxes to the countries of origin of its revenues. Google between 2007 and 2010 saved $3.1 billion in taxes by shuttling non-U.S. profits through <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>. Such techniques lower its non-U.S. tax rate to 2.3 per cent, while normally the corporate tax rate in for instance the UK is 28 per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup> This has reportedly sparked a French investigation into Google's <a href="/wiki/Transfer_pricing" title="Transfer pricing">transfer pricing</a> practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Following criticism of the amount of corporate taxes that Google paid in the United Kingdom, Chairman Eric Schmidt said, "It's called capitalism. We are proudly capitalistic." During the same December 2012 interview, Schmidt confirmed that the company had no intention of paying more to the UK exchequer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[366]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Matt_Brittin" title="Matt Brittin">Matt Brittin</a> testified to the <a href="/wiki/Public_Accounts_Committee_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public Accounts Committee (United Kingdom)">Public Accounts Committee</a> of the UK House of Commons that his UK sales team made no sales and hence owed no sales taxes to the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[367]</a></sup> In January 2016, Google reached a settlement with the UK to pay £130m in back taxes plus higher taxes in future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[368]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2017, Google channeled $22.7 billion from the Netherlands to Bermuda to reduce its tax bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[369]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Since  2007,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Google has aimed for carbon neutrality in regard to its operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[370]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google disclosed in September 2011 that it "continuously uses enough electricity to power 200,000 homes", almost 260 million watts or about a quarter of the output of a nuclear power plant. Total carbon emissions for 2010 were just under 1.5 million metric tons, mostly due to fossil fuels that provide electricity for the data centers. Google said that 25 percent of its energy was supplied by renewable fuels in 2010. An average search uses only 0.3 watt-hours of electricity, so all global searches are only 12.5 million watts or 5% of the total electricity consumption by Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[371]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2007, Google launched a project centered on developing renewable energy, titled the "Renewable Energy Cheaper than Coal (RE&lt;C)" project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[372]</a></sup> However, the project was canceled in 2014, after engineers Ross Koningstein and David Fork understood, after years of study, that "best-case scenario, which was based on our most optimistic forecasts for renewable energy, would still result in severe climate change", writing that they "came to the conclusion that even if Google and others had led the way toward a wholesale adoption of renewable energy, that switch would not have resulted in significant reductions in carbon dioxide emissions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[373]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In June 2013, <i>The Washington Post</i> reported that Google had donated $50,000 to the <a href="/wiki/Competitive_Enterprise_Institute" title="Competitive Enterprise Institute">Competitive Enterprise Institute</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> that calls human carbon emissions a positive factor in the environment and argues that global warming is not a concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[374]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In July 2013, it was reported that Google had hosted a fundraising event for Oklahoma Senator <a href="/wiki/Jim_Inhofe" title="Jim Inhofe">Jim Inhofe</a>, who has called climate change a "hoax".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup> In 2014 Google cut ties with the <a href="/wiki/American_Legislative_Exchange_Council" title="American Legislative Exchange Council">American Legislative Exchange Council</a> (ALEC) after pressure from the Sierra Club, major unions and Google's own scientists because of ALEC's stance on climate change and opposition to renewable energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LA_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-LA-376">[376]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In November 2017, Google bought 536 megawatts of wind power. The purchase made the firm reach 100% renewable energy. The wind energy comes from two power plants in South Dakota, one in Iowa and one in Oklahoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2013, Google ranked 5th in <a href="/wiki/Lobbying_in_the_United_States" title="Lobbying in the United States">lobbying</a> spending, up from 213th in 2003. In 2012, the company ranked 2nd in campaign donations of technology and Internet sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lobby1_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-lobby1-378">[378]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google has been involved in a number of lawsuits including the <a href="/wiki/High-Tech_Employee_Antitrust_Litigation" title="High-Tech Employee Antitrust Litigation">High-Tech Employee Antitrust Litigation</a> which resulted in Google being one of four companies to pay a $415 million settlement to employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[379]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On June 27, 2017, the company received a record fine of <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">€</a>2.42 billion from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> for "promoting its own shopping comparison service at the top of search results."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup> Commenting on the penalty, <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> magazine said: "The hefty sum – the largest ever doled out by the EU's competition regulators – will sting in the short term, but Google can handle it. Alphabet, Google’s parent company, made a profit of $2.5 billion (€2.2 billion) in the first six weeks of 2017 alone. The real impact of the ruling is that Google must stop using its dominance as a search engine to give itself the edge in another market: online price comparisons." The company disputed the ruling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[381]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On July 18, 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[382]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Competition" title="European Commissioner for Competition">European Commission</a> fined Google €4.34 billion for breaching EU antitrust rules. The abuse of dominant position has been referred to Google's constraint applied on Android device manufacturers and network operators to ensure that traffic on Android devices goes to the Google search engine. On October 9, 2018, Google confirmed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[383]</a></sup> that it had appealed the fine to the <a href="/wiki/General_Court_(European_Union)" title="General Court (European Union)">General Court</a> of the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[384]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On January 21, 2019, French data regulator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CNIL" title="CNIL">CNIL</a> imposed a record €50 million fine on Google for breaching the European Union’s <a href="/wiki/General_Data_Protection_Regulation" title="General Data Protection Regulation">General Data Protection Regulation</a>. The judgment claimed Google had failed to sufficiently inform users of its methods for collecting data to personalize advertising. Google issued a statement saying it was “deeply committed” to transparency and was “studying the decision” before determining its response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[385]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's market dominance has led to prominent media coverage, including <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google" title="Criticism of Google">criticism of the company</a> over issues such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_tax_avoidance" title="Google tax avoidance">aggressive tax avoidance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[386]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google#Page_rank" title="Criticism of Google">search neutrality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google#Copyright_issues" title="Criticism of Google">copyright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Censorship_by_Google" title="Censorship by Google">censorship</a> of search results and content,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[387]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_privacy" title="Google privacy">privacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[389]</a></sup> Other criticisms include alleged misuse and manipulation of search results, its use of others' <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a>, concerns that its <a href="/wiki/Data_collection" title="Data collection">compilation of data</a> may violate people's <a href="/wiki/Internet_privacy" title="Internet privacy">privacy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Energy_consumption" title="Energy consumption">energy consumption</a> of its servers, as well as concerns over traditional business issues such as <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Restraint_of_trade" title="Restraint of trade">restraint of trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-competitive_practices" title="Anti-competitive practices">anti-competitive practices</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Patent_infringement" title="Patent infringement">patent infringement</a>.
</p>
<p>Former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deputy_Defense_Secretary" title="Deputy Defense Secretary">Deputy Defense Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_O._Work" title="Robert O. Work">Robert O. Work</a> in 2018 criticizes Google and its employees have stepped into a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_Hazard" title="Moral Hazard">Moral Hazard</a></i> for themselves as not continuing Pentagon's <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> project while helping the autocratic communist China's AI technology that could be used against the United States in a conflict. He described Google as hypocritical, given it has opened an AI center in China and “Anything that’s going on in the AI center in China is going to the Chinese government and then will ultimately end up in the hands of the Chinese military." Work said. “I didn’t see any Google employee saying, ‘Hmm, maybe we shouldn’t do that.'” Google's dealings with China is decrying as unpatriotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[390]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[391]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[392]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[393]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google adhered to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Internet censorship in the People's Republic of China">Internet censorship policies of China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[394]</a></sup> enforced by means of filters colloquially known as "The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Firewall_of_China" title="Great Firewall of China">Great Firewall of China</a>". <i>The Intercept</i> reported in August 2018 that Google is developing for the people's Republic of China a censored version of its search engine (known as <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly_(search_engine)" title="Dragonfly (search engine)">Dragonfly</a>) "that will blacklist websites and search terms about human rights, democracy, religion, and peaceful protest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[395]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[396]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> General <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dunford" title="Joseph Dunford">Joseph Dunford</a> also criticizes Google as "it’s inexplicable" that it continue investing in autocratic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">communist China</a>, who uses <a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_China" title="Internet censorship in China">censorship technology to restrain freedoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_China" title="Mass surveillance in China">crackdown people</a> there and has long history of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> theft which hurts U.S. companies, while simultaneously not renewing further research and development collaborations with <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">the Pentagon</a>. He said “I’m not sure that people at Google will enjoy a world order that is informed by the norms and standards of Russia or China.” He urges Google work directly with the U.S. government instead of making controversial inroads into China. Senator <a href="/wiki/Mark_Warner" title="Mark Warner">Mark Warner</a> (D-VA) criticized Dragonfly evidences China's success at "recruit[ing] Western companies to their information control efforts" while China exports cyber and censorship infrastructure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> regimes like Venezuela, Ethiopia, and Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-397"><a href="#cite_note-397">[397]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-398"><a href="#cite_note-398">[398]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[399]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's <a href="/wiki/Mission_statement" title="Mission statement">mission statement</a>, from the outset, was "to organize the world's information and make it universally accessible and useful",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-400"><a href="#cite_note-400">[400]</a></sup> and its unofficial slogan is "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_be_evil" title="Don't be evil">Don't be evil</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-401"><a href="#cite_note-401">[401]</a></sup> In October 2015, a related motto was adopted in the Alphabet corporate code of conduct by the phrase: "Do the right thing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[402]</a></sup> The original motto was retained in the code of conduct of Google, now a subsidiary of Alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's commitment to such robust idealism has been increasingly called into doubt due to a number of the firm's actions and behaviors which appear to contradict this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[403]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[404]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Following media reports about <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM</a>, NSA's massive electronic <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance" title="Mass surveillance">surveillance program</a>, in June 2013, several technology companies were identified as participants, including Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-405"><a href="#cite_note-405">[405]</a></sup> According to leaks of said program, Google joined the PRISM program in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-406"><a href="#cite_note-406">[406]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On August 8, 2017, Google fired employee James Damore after he distributed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_memo" title="Google memo">a memo</a> throughout the company which argued that "Google's ideological echo chamber" and bias clouded their thinking about diversity and inclusion, and that it is also biological factors, not discrimination alone, that cause the average woman to be less interested than men in technical positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTFired_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTFired-407">[407]</a></sup> Google CEO Sundar Pichai accused Damore in violating company policy by "advancing harmful gender stereotypes in our workplace", and he was fired on the same day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-408"><a href="#cite_note-408">[408]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-409"><a href="#cite_note-409">[409]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-410"><a href="#cite_note-410">[410]</a></sup> <i>New York Times</i> columnist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Brooks_(political_commentator)" title="David Brooks (political commentator)">David Brooks</a> argued Pichai had mishandled the case, and called for his resignation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrooksNYT_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrooksNYT-411">[411]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-412"><a href="#cite_note-412">[412]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Reportedly, Google's influenced <a href="/wiki/New_America_(organization)" title="New America (organization)">New America</a> think tank to expel their Open Markets research group, after the group has criticized Google monopolistic power and supported the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_vs._Google" title="European Union vs. Google">EU $2.7B fine of Google</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-413"><a href="#cite_note-413">[413]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-414"><a href="#cite_note-414">[414]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google has worked with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> on drone software through the 2017 "Project Maven" that could be used to improve the accuracy of <a href="/wiki/Drone_strike" title="Drone strike">drone strikes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-415"><a href="#cite_note-415">[415]</a></sup> Thousands of Google employees, including senior engineers, have signed a letter urging Google CEO <a href="/wiki/Sundar_Pichai" title="Sundar Pichai">Sundar Pichai</a> to end a controversial contract with the Pentagon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-416"><a href="#cite_note-416">[416]</a></sup> In response to the backlash, Google ultimately decided to not renew their DoD contract, set to expire in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-417"><a href="#cite_note-417">[417]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2017, David Elliot and Chris Gillespie argued before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ninth_Circuit_of_the_United_States_Court_of_Appeals" title="Ninth Circuit of the United States Court of Appeals">Ninth Circuit of the United States Court of Appeals</a> that "google" had suffered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genericide" title="Genericide">genericide</a>. The controversy began in 2012 when Gillespie acquired 763 domain names containing the word "google." Google promptly filed a complaint with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Arbitration_Forum" title="National Arbitration Forum">National Arbitration Forum</a> (NAF). Elliot then filed a petition for canceling the Google trademark. Ultimately, the court ruled in favor of Google because Elliot failed to show a preponderance of evidence showing the genericide of "google."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-418"><a href="#cite_note-418">[418]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On 10 December 2018, a New Zealand court ordered that the name of a man accused of murdering British traveller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grace_Millane" title="Grace Millane">Grace Millane</a> be withheld from the public. The next morning, Google named the man in an email it sent people who had subscribed to "what's trending in New Zealand".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-419"><a href="#cite_note-419">[419]</a></sup> Lawyers warned that this could compromise the trial, and Justice Minister Andrew Little said that Google was in contempt of court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-420"><a href="#cite_note-420">[420]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-421"><a href="#cite_note-421">[421]</a></sup> Google said that it had been unaware of the court order, and that the email had been created by algorithms.
</p>
<p>On 21 January 2019, Google was fined €50 million (US$57 million) for violating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDPR" title="GDPR">GDPR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-422"><a href="#cite_note-422">[422]</a></sup>  It is the biggest fine issued by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CNIL" title="CNIL">CNIL</a> for violation of the regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-423"><a href="#cite_note-423">[423]</a></sup>
</p>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>Google LLC</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> is an American multinational technology company that specializes in Internet-related services and products, which include <a href="/wiki/Online_advertising" title="Online advertising">online advertising technologies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Search_engine" title="Search engine">search engine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud computing</a>, software, and hardware. It is considered one of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Four_tech_companies" title="Big Four tech companies">Big Four</a> technology companies, alongside <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google was founded in 1998 by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Brin" title="Sergey Brin">Sergey Brin</a> while they were Ph.D. students at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. Together they own about 14 percent of its shares and control 56 percent of the stockholder voting power through <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">supervoting</a> stock. They incorporated Google as a privately held company on September 4, 1998. An initial public offering (IPO) took place on August 19, 2004, and Google moved to its headquarters in Mountain View, California, nicknamed the <a href="/wiki/Googleplex" title="Googleplex">Googleplex</a>. In August 2015, Google announced plans to reorganize its various interests as a conglomerate called <a href="/wiki/Alphabet_Inc." title="Alphabet Inc.">Alphabet Inc.</a> Google is Alphabet's leading subsidiary and will continue to be the umbrella company for Alphabet's Internet interests. <a href="/wiki/Sundar_Pichai" title="Sundar Pichai">Sundar Pichai</a> was appointed CEO of Google, replacing Larry Page who became the CEO of Alphabet.
</p>
<p>The company's rapid growth since incorporation has triggered a chain of products, acquisitions, and partnerships beyond Google's core search engine (<a href="/wiki/Google_Search" title="Google Search">Google Search</a>). It offers services designed for work and productivity (<a href="/wiki/Google_Docs" title="Google Docs">Google Docs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Sheets" title="Google Sheets">Google Sheets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Google_Slides" title="Google Slides">Google Slides</a>), email (<a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a>/<a href="/wiki/Inbox_by_Gmail" title="Inbox by Gmail">Inbox</a>), scheduling and time management (<a href="/wiki/Google_Calendar" title="Google Calendar">Google Calendar</a>), cloud storage (<a href="/wiki/Google_Drive" title="Google Drive">Google Drive</a>), social networking (<a href="/wiki/Google%2B" title="Google+">Google+</a>), instant messaging and video chat (<a href="/wiki/Google_Allo" title="Google Allo">Google Allo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Duo" title="Google Duo">Duo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hangouts" title="Hangouts">Hangouts</a>), language translation (<a href="/wiki/Google_Translate" title="Google Translate">Google Translate</a>), mapping and navigation (<a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">Google Maps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waze" title="Waze">Waze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Earth" title="Google Earth">Google Earth</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Street_View" title="Street View">Street View</a>), video sharing (<a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>), note-taking (<a href="/wiki/Google_Keep" title="Google Keep">Google Keep</a>), and photo organizing and editing (<a href="/wiki/Google_Photos" title="Google Photos">Google Photos</a>). The company leads the development of the <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> mobile operating system, the <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> web browser, and <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">Chrome OS</a>, a lightweight operating system based on the Chrome browser. Google has moved increasingly into hardware; from 2010 to 2015, it partnered with major electronics manufacturers in the production of its <a href="/wiki/Google_Nexus" title="Google Nexus">Nexus</a> devices, and it released multiple hardware products in October 2016, including the <a href="/wiki/Pixel_(smartphone)" title="Pixel (smartphone)">Google Pixel</a> smartphone, <a href="/wiki/Google_Home" title="Google Home">Google Home</a> smart speaker, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Wifi" title="Google Wifi">Google Wifi</a> mesh wireless router, and <a href="/wiki/Google_Daydream" title="Google Daydream">Google Daydream</a> virtual reality headset. Google has also experimented with becoming an Internet carrier (<a href="/wiki/Google_Fiber" title="Google Fiber">Google Fiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Fi" title="Google Fi">Project Fi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Google_Station" title="Google Station">Google Station</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google.com is the most visited website in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexa_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexa-9">[9]</a></sup> Several other Google services also figure in the top 100 most visited websites, including <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blogger_(service)" title="Blogger (service)">Blogger</a>. Google is the most valuable brand in the world as of  2017,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> but has received significant <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google" title="Criticism of Google">criticism</a> involving issues such as <a href="/wiki/Privacy_concerns_regarding_Google" title="Privacy concerns regarding Google">privacy concerns</a>, tax avoidance, antitrust, <a href="/wiki/Censorship_by_Google" title="Censorship by Google">censorship</a>, and search neutrality. Google's <a href="/wiki/Mission_statement" title="Mission statement">mission statement</a> is "to organize the world's information and make it universally accessible and useful", and its unofficial slogan was "Don't be evil" until the phrase was removed from the company's code of conduct around May 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<p> Google began in January 1996 as a research project by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Brin" title="Sergey Brin">Sergey Brin</a> when they were both PhD students at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stanford,_California" title="Stanford, California">Stanford, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milestones_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-milestones-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>While conventional search engines ranked results by counting how many times the search terms appeared on the page, the two theorized about a better system that analyzed the relationships among websites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> They called this new technology <a href="/wiki/PageRank" title="PageRank">PageRank</a>; it determined a website's <a href="/wiki/Relevance_(information_retrieval)" title="Relevance (information retrieval)">relevance</a> by the number of pages, and the importance of those pages that linked back to the original site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Page and Brin originally nicknamed their new search engine "BackRub", because the system checked backlinks to estimate the importance of a site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Eventually, they changed the name to Google; the name of the search engine originated from a misspelling of the word "<a href="/wiki/Googol" title="Googol">googol</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> the number 1 followed by 100 zeros, which was picked to signify that the search engine was intended to provide large quantities of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Originally, Google ran under Stanford University's website, with the domains <i>google.stanford.edu</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and <i>z.stanford.edu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>The domain name for Google was registered on September 15, 1997,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and the company was incorporated on September 4, 1998. It was based in the garage of a friend (<a href="/wiki/Susan_Wojcicki" title="Susan Wojcicki">Susan Wojcicki</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milestones_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-milestones-14">[14]</a></sup>) in <a href="/wiki/Menlo_Park,_California" title="Menlo Park, California">Menlo Park, California</a>. <a href="/wiki/Craig_Silverstein" title="Craig Silverstein">Craig Silverstein</a>, a fellow PhD student at Stanford, was hired as the first employee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milestones_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-milestones-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google was initially funded by an August 1998 contribution of $100,000 from <a href="/wiki/Andy_Bechtolsheim" title="Andy Bechtolsheim">Andy Bechtolsheim</a>, co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun Microsystems</a>; the money was given before Google was incorporated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bechtolsheim_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bechtolsheim-30">[30]</a></sup> Google received money from three other <a href="/wiki/Angel_investor" title="Angel investor">angel investors</a> in 1998: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a>, Stanford University computer science professor <a href="/wiki/David_Cheriton" title="David Cheriton">David Cheriton</a>, and entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Ram_Shriram" title="Ram Shriram">Ram Shriram</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1998_Investments_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-1998_Investments-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>After some additional, small investments through the end of 1998 to early 1999,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1998_Investments_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-1998_Investments-31">[31]</a></sup> a new $25 million round of funding was announced on June 7, 1999,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> with major investors including the <a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">venture capital</a> firms <a href="/wiki/Kleiner_Perkins" title="Kleiner Perkins">Kleiner Perkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sequoia_Capital" title="Sequoia Capital">Sequoia Capital</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bechtolsheim_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bechtolsheim-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Early in 1999, Brin and Page decided they wanted to sell Google to <a href="/wiki/Excite" title="Excite">Excite</a>. They went to Excite CEO George Bell and offered to sell it to him for $1 million. He rejected the offer. <a href="/wiki/Vinod_Khosla" title="Vinod Khosla">Vinod Khosla</a>, one of Excite's venture capitalists, talked the duo down to $750,000, but Bell still rejected it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> (IPO) took place five years later, on August 19, 2004. At that time Larry Page, Sergey Brin, and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Schmidt" title="Eric Schmidt">Eric Schmidt</a> agreed to work together at Google for 20 years, until the year 2024.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>At IPO, the company offered 19,605,052 shares at a price of $85 per share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPO_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPO-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Shares were sold in an online auction format using a system built by <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Credit_Suisse" title="Credit Suisse">Credit Suisse</a>, underwriters for the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The sale of $1.67 bn (billion) gave Google a <a href="/wiki/Market_capitalization" title="Market capitalization">market capitalization</a> of more than $23bn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washpost_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-washpost-39">[39]</a></sup> By January 2014, its market capitalization had grown to $397bn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marketwatch_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marketwatch-40">[40]</a></sup> The vast majority of the 271 million shares remained under the control of Google, and many Google employees became instant paper millionaires. <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>, a competitor of Google, also benefitted because it owned 8.4 million shares of Google before the IPO took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yahooshares_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-yahooshares-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>There were concerns that Google's IPO would lead to changes in company culture. Reasons ranged from shareholder pressure for employee benefit reductions to the fact that many company executives would become instant paper millionaires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> As a reply to this concern, co-founders Brin and Page promised in a report to potential investors that the IPO would not change the company's culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2005, articles in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and other sources began suggesting that Google had lost its anti-corporate, no evil philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
In an effort to maintain the company's unique culture, Google designated a Chief Culture Officer, who also serves as the Director of Human Resources. The purpose of the Chief Culture Officer is to develop and maintain the culture and work on ways to keep true to the core values that the company was founded on: a flat organization with a collaborative environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCO_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCO-48">[48]</a></sup> Google has also faced allegations of <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ageism" title="Ageism">ageism</a> from former employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In 2013, a <a href="/wiki/High-Tech_Employee_Antitrust_Litigation" title="High-Tech Employee Antitrust Litigation">class action against</a> several <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a> companies, including Google, was filed for alleged "no cold call" agreements which restrained the recruitment of high-tech employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>The stock performed well after the IPO, with shares hitting $350 for the first time on October 31, 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> primarily because of strong sales and earnings in the <a href="/wiki/Online_advertising" title="Online advertising">online advertising</a> market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowlingforgoogle_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowlingforgoogle-53">[53]</a></sup> The surge in stock price was fueled mainly by individual investors, as opposed to large institutional investors and <a href="/wiki/Mutual_fund" title="Mutual fund">mutual funds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowlingforgoogle_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-bowlingforgoogle-53">[53]</a></sup> GOOG shares split into GOOG <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_C_share" title="Class C share">class C shares</a> and GOOGL <a href="/wiki/Class_A_share" title="Class A share">class A shares</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The company is listed on the <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a> stock exchange under the <a href="/wiki/Ticker_symbol" title="Ticker symbol">ticker symbols</a> GOOGL and GOOG, and on the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Stock_Exchange" title="Frankfurt Stock Exchange">Frankfurt Stock Exchange</a> under the ticker symbol GGQ1. These ticker symbols now refer to Alphabet Inc., Google's holding company, since the fourth quarter of 2015.<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In March 1999, the company moved its offices to <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto,_California" title="Palo Alto, California">Palo Alto, California</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> which is home to several prominent <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a> technology start-ups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The next year, Google began selling advertisements associated with search keywords against Page and Brin's initial opposition toward an advertising-funded search engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milestones_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-milestones-14">[14]</a></sup> To maintain an uncluttered page design, advertisements were solely text-based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>This model of selling keyword advertising was first pioneered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goto.com" title="Goto.com">Goto.com</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Idealab" title="Idealab">Idealab</a> spin-off created by <a href="/wiki/Bill_T._Gross" title="Bill T. Gross">Bill Gross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goto_strong_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-goto_strong-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnet_p4p_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnet_p4p-61">[61]</a></sup> When the company changed names to Overture Services, it sued Google over alleged infringements of the company's pay-per-click and bidding patents. Overture Services would later be bought by Yahoo! and renamed <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Search_Marketing" title="Yahoo! Search Marketing">Yahoo! Search Marketing</a>. The case was then settled out of court; Google agreed to issue shares of common stock to Yahoo! in exchange for a perpetual license.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In June 2000, it was announced that Google would become the default search engine provider for <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>, one of the most popular websites at the time, replacing <a href="/wiki/Inktomi" title="Inktomi">Inktomi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2001, Google received a patent for its PageRank mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patent_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-patent-65">[65]</a></sup> The patent was officially assigned to Stanford University and lists Lawrence Page as the inventor. In 2003, after outgrowing two other locations, the company leased an office complex from <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Graphics" title="Silicon Graphics">Silicon Graphics</a>, at 1600 Amphitheatre Parkway in <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sgibldg_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-sgibldg-66">[66]</a></sup> The complex became known as the <a href="/wiki/Googleplex" title="Googleplex">Googleplex</a>, a play on the word <a href="/wiki/Googolplex" title="Googolplex">googolplex</a>, the number one followed by a googol zeroes. The <a href="/wiki/Googleplex" title="Googleplex">Googleplex</a> interiors were designed by <a href="/wiki/Clive_Wilkinson" title="Clive Wilkinson">Clive Wilkinson</a> Architects. Three years later, Google bought the property from SGI for $319 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-googleplexpurchase_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-googleplexpurchase-67">[67]</a></sup> By that time, the name "Google" had found its way into everyday language, causing the verb "<a href="/wiki/Google_(verb)" title="Google (verb)">google</a>" to be added to the <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster" title="Merriam-Webster">Merriam-Webster</a> Collegiate Dictionary</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>, denoted as: "to use the Google search engine to obtain information on the Internet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The first use of "Google" as a verb in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pop_culture" title="Pop culture">pop culture</a> happened on the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Buffy_the_Vampire_Slayer" title="Buffy the Vampire Slayer">Buffy the Vampire Slayer</a></i>, in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2005, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> reported on a 700 percent increase in third-quarter profit for Google, largely thanks to large companies shifting their advertising strategies from newspapers, magazines, and television to the Internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In January 2008, all the data that passed through Google's MapReduce software component had an aggregated size of 20 <a href="/wiki/Petabyte" title="Petabyte">petabytes</a> per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 2009, a <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> report about top political searches of 2009 noted that "more than a billion searches" are being typed into Google on a daily basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In May 2011, the number of monthly unique visitors to Google surpassed one billion for the first time, an 8.4 percent increase from May 2010 (931 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>The year 2012 was the first time that Google generated $50 billion in annual revenue, generating $38 billion the previous year. In January 2013, then-CEO Larry Page commented, "We ended 2012 with a strong quarter ... Revenues were up 36% year-on-year, and 8% quarter-on-quarter. And we hit $50 billion in revenues for the first time last year – not a bad achievement in just a decade and a half."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In November 2018, Google announced its plan to expand its New York City office to a capacity of 12,000 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google announced the launch of a new company, called <a href="/wiki/Calico_(company)" title="Calico (company)">Calico</a>, on September 19, 2013, to be led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple,_Inc." title="Apple, Inc.">Apple, Inc.</a> chairman <a href="/wiki/Arthur_D._Levinson" title="Arthur D. Levinson">Arthur Levinson</a>. In the official public statement, Page explained that the "health and well-being" company would focus on "the challenge of ageing and associated diseases".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google celebrated its 15-year anniversary on September 27, 2013, and in 2016 it celebrated its 18th birthday with an animated version of its logo (a "<a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a>"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> although it has used other dates for its official birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The reason for the choice of September 27 remains unclear, and a dispute with rival search engine <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Search" title="Yahoo! Search">Yahoo! Search</a> in 2005 has been suggested as the cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Affordable_Internet" title="Alliance for Affordable Internet">Alliance for Affordable Internet</a> (A4AI) was launched in October 2013; Google is part of the coalition of public and private organizations that also includes <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>. Led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Tim_Berners-Lee" title="Sir Tim Berners-Lee">Sir Tim Berners-Lee</a>, the A4AI seeks to make Internet access more affordable so that access is broadened in the developing world, where only 31% of people are online. Google will help to decrease Internet access prices so they fall below the UN Broadband Commission's worldwide target of 5% of monthly income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>The corporation's consolidated revenue for the third quarter of 2013 was reported in mid-October 2013 as $14.89 billion, a 12 percent increase compared to the previous quarter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Google's Internet business was responsible for $10.8 billion of this total, with an increase in the number of users' clicks on advertisements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Interbrand" title="Interbrand">Interbrand</a>'s annual Best Global Brands report, Google has been the second most valuable brand in the world (behind <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a>) in 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and 2016, with a valuation of $133 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In September 2015, Google engineering manager Rachel Potvin revealed details about Google's software code at an engineering conference. She revealed that the entire Google codebase, which spans every single service it develops, consists of over 2 billion lines of code. All that code is stored in a code repository available to all 25,000 Google engineers, and the code is regularly copied and updated on 10 Google data centers. To keep control, Potvin said Google has built its own "version control system", called "Piper", and that "when you start a new project, you have a wealth of libraries already available to you. Almost everything has already been done." Engineers can make a single code change and deploy it on all services at the same time. The only major exceptions are that the PageRank search results algorithm is stored separately with only specific employee access, and the code for the Android operating system and the Google Chrome browser are also stored separately, as they don't run on the Internet. The "Piper" system spans 85 <a href="/wiki/Terabyte" title="Terabyte">TB</a> of data. Google engineers make 25,000 changes to the code each day and on a weekly basis change approximately 15 million lines of code across 250,000 files. With that much code, automated bots have to help. Potvin reported, "You need to make a concerted effort to maintain code health. And this is not just humans maintaining code health, but robots too.” Bots aren't writing code, but generating a lot of the data and configuration files needed to run the company's software. "Not only is the size of the repository increasing," Potvin explained, "but the rate of change is also increasing. This is an exponential curve."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>As of  October 2016,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Google operates 70 offices in more than 40 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexa_Internet" title="Alexa Internet">Alexa</a>, a company that monitors commercial web traffic, lists Google.com as the most visited website in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexa_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alexa-9">[9]</a></sup> Several other Google services also figure in the top 100 most visited websites, including YouTube<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and Blogger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2001, Google acquired <a href="/wiki/Google_Groups#Deja_News" title="Google Groups">Deja News</a>, the operators of a large archive of materials from <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Google rebranded the archive as <a href="/wiki/Google_Groups" title="Google Groups">Google Groups</a>, and by the end of the year, it had expanded the history back to 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feeling-lucky-book_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-feeling-lucky-book-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In April 2003, Google acquired <a href="/wiki/AdSense#History" title="AdSense">Applied Semantics</a>, a company specializing in making software applications for the online advertising space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/AdSense" title="AdSense">AdSense</a> contextual advertising technology developed by Applied Semantics was adopted into Google's advertising efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feeling-lucky-book_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-feeling-lucky-book-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2004, Google acquired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keyhole,_Inc" title="Keyhole, Inc">Keyhole, Inc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Keyhole's <a href="/wiki/Eponym" title="Eponym">eponymous</a> product was later renamed <a href="/wiki/Google_Earth" title="Google Earth">Google Earth</a>.
</p>
<p>In April 2005, Google acquired <a href="/wiki/Urchin_(software)" title="Urchin (software)">Urchin Software</a>, using their Urchin on Demand product (along with ideas from Adaptive Path's Measure Map) to create <a href="/wiki/Google_Analytics" title="Google Analytics">Google Analytics</a> in 2006.
</p>
<p>In October 2006, Google announced that it had acquired the video-sharing site YouTube for $1.65 billion in Google stock,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and the deal was finalized on November 13, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On April 13, 2007, Google reached an agreement to acquire <a href="/wiki/DoubleClick" title="DoubleClick">DoubleClick</a> for $3.1 billion, transferring to Google valuable relationships that DoubleClick had with Web publishers and advertising agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
The deal was approved despite anti-trust concerns raised by competitors <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> and <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In addition to the many companies Google has purchased, the firm has partnered with other organizations for research, advertising, and other activities. In 2005, Google partnered with <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a> to build 1,000,000 square feet (93,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaames_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasaames-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2005 Google partnered with <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">AOL</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-googleaol_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-googleaol-111">[111]</a></sup> to enhance each other's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_search" title="Video search">video search</a> services. In 2006 Google and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Sports_Digital_Media" title="Fox Sports Digital Media">Fox Interactive Media</a> of <a href="/wiki/News_Corporation" title="News Corporation">News Corporation</a> entered into a $900 million agreement to provide search and advertising on the then-popular social networking site <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MySpace" title="MySpace">MySpace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2007, Google began sponsoring <a href="/wiki/NORAD_Tracks_Santa" title="NORAD Tracks Santa">NORAD Tracks Santa</a>, displacing the former sponsor AOL. NORAD Tracks Santa purports to follow <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a>' progress on <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Eve" title="Christmas Eve">Christmas Eve</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tracking_Santa:_NORAD_&amp;_Google_Team_Up_For_Christmas,_December_1,_2007,_Danny_Sullivan_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tracking_Santa:_NORAD_&amp;_Google_Team_Up_For_Christmas,_December_1,_2007,_Danny_Sullivan-113">[113]</a></sup> using Google Earth to "track Santa" in 3-D for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behind_the_scenes:_NORAD's_Santa_tracker_for_Thur,_December_21,_2009_By_Daniel_Terdiman,_CNET_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behind_the_scenes:_NORAD's_Santa_tracker_for_Thur,_December_21,_2009_By_Daniel_Terdiman,_CNET-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Instructions_On_Tracking_Santa_With_NORAD_&amp;_Google:_The_2007_Edition,_December_24,_2007,_Danny_Sullivan_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Instructions_On_Tracking_Santa_With_NORAD_&amp;_Google:_The_2007_Edition,_December_24,_2007,_Danny_Sullivan-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2008, Google developed a partnership with <a href="/wiki/GeoEye" title="GeoEye">GeoEye</a> to launch a satellite providing Google with high-resolution (0.41 m monochrome, 1.65 m color) imagery for Google Earth. The satellite was launched from <a href="/wiki/Vandenberg_Air_Force_Base" title="Vandenberg Air Force Base">Vandenberg Air Force Base</a> on September 6, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Google also announced in 2008 that it was hosting an archive of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life Magazine</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s photographs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2010, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Energy" title="Google Energy">Google Energy</a> made its first investment in a <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> project, putting $38.8 million into two <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>. The company announced the two locations will generate 169.5 megawatts of power, enough to supply 55,000 homes. The farms, which were developed by <a href="/wiki/NextEra_Energy_Resources" title="NextEra Energy Resources">NextEra Energy Resources</a>, will reduce fossil fuel use in the region and return profits. NextEra Energy Resources sold Google a twenty-percent stake in the project to get funding for its development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In February 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Energy_Regulatory_Commission" title="Federal Energy Regulatory Commission">Federal Energy Regulatory Commission</a> FERC granted Google an authorization to buy and sell energy at market rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The order specifically states that Google Energy—a subsidiary of Google—holds the rights "for the sale of energy, capacity, and ancillary services at market-based rates", but acknowledges that neither Google Energy nor its affiliates "own or control any generation or transmission" facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The corporation exercised this authorization in September 2013 when it announced it would purchase all the electricity produced by the not-yet-built 240-megawatt Happy Hereford wind farm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Also in 2010, Google purchased <a href="/wiki/Global_IP_Solutions" title="Global IP Solutions">Global IP Solutions</a>, a Norway-based company that provides web-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleconferencing" title="Teleconferencing">teleconferencing</a> and other related services. This acquisition enabled Google to add telephone-style services to its list of products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> On May 27, 2010, Google announced it had also closed the acquisition of the mobile ad network <a href="/wiki/AdMob" title="AdMob">AdMob</a>. This occurred days after the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> closed its investigation into the purchase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Google acquired the company for an undisclosed amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In July 2010, Google signed an agreement with an Iowa wind farm to buy 114 megawatts of energy for 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wind_energy_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-wind_energy-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On April 4, 2011, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Globe_and_Mail" title="The Globe and Mail">The Globe and Mail</a></i> reported that Google bid $900 million for 6000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nortel_Networks" title="Nortel Networks">Nortel Networks</a> patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On August 15, 2011, Google made its largest-ever acquisition to date when it announced that it would acquire <a href="/wiki/Motorola_Mobility" title="Motorola Mobility">Motorola Mobility</a> for $12.5 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> subject to approval from regulators in the United States and Europe. In a post on Google's blog, Google Chief Executive and co-founder Larry Page revealed that the acquisition was a strategic move to strengthen Google's patent portfolio.
