<p>In May 2017, Google enabled a new "Personal" tab in Google Search, letting users search for content in their Google accounts' various services, including email messages from <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a> and photos from <a href="/wiki/Google_Photos" title="Google Photos">Google Photos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/G_Suite" title="G Suite">G Suite</a> is a monthly subscription offering for organizations and businesses to get access to a collection of Google's services, including <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Drive" title="Google Drive">Google Drive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Docs" title="Google Docs">Google Docs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Sheets" title="Google Sheets">Google Sheets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Slides" title="Google Slides">Google Slides</a>, with additional administrative tools, unique domain names, and 24/7 support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Google_Search_Appliance" title="Google Search Appliance">Google Search Appliance</a> was launched in February 2002, targeted toward providing search technology for larger organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milestones_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-milestones-14">[14]</a></sup> Google launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Mini" title="Google Mini">Mini</a> three years later, which was targeted at smaller organizations. Late in 2006, Google began to sell Custom Search Business Edition, providing customers with an advertising-free window into Google.com's index. The service was renamed Google Site Search in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csbe2gss_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-csbe2gss-204">[204]</a></sup> Site Search customers were notified by email in late March 2017 that no new licenses for Site Search would be sold after April 1, 2017, but that customer and technical support would be provided for the duration of existing license agreements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On March 15, 2016, Google announced the introduction of Google Analytics 360 Suite, "a set of integrated data and marketing analytics products, designed specifically for the needs of enterprise-class marketers" which can be integrated with <a href="/wiki/BigQuery" title="BigQuery">BigQuery</a> on the Google Cloud Platform. Among other things, the suite is designed to help "enterprise class marketers" "see the complete customer journey", generate "useful insights", and "deliver engaging experiences to the right people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> Jack Marshall of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> wrote that the suite competes with existing marketing cloud offerings by companies including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adobe_Systems" title="Adobe Systems">Adobe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">Oracle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salesforce.com" title="Salesforce.com">Salesforce</a>, and <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On September 24, 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Google launched <a href="/wiki/Google_for_Entrepreneurs" title="Google for Entrepreneurs">Google for Entrepreneurs</a>, a largely not-for-profit <a href="/wiki/Business_incubator" title="Business incubator">business incubator</a> providing startups with <a href="/wiki/Coworking" title="Coworking">co-working spaces</a> known as Campuses, with assistance to startup founders that may include workshops, conferences, and mentorships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Presently, there are 7 Campus locations in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv" title="Tel Aviv">Tel Aviv</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>.
</p>
<p>Google offers <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a>, and the newer variant <a href="/wiki/Inbox_by_Gmail" title="Inbox by Gmail">Inbox</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">email</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Calendar" title="Google Calendar">Google Calendar</a> for time-management and scheduling,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">Google Maps</a> for mapping, navigation and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">satellite imagery</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Drive" title="Google Drive">Google Drive</a> for <a href="/wiki/File_hosting_service" title="File hosting service">cloud storage</a> of files,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-drive-announced_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-drive-announced-215">[215]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Docs" title="Google Docs">Google Docs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Sheets" title="Google Sheets">Sheets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Slides" title="Google Slides">Slides</a> for productivity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-drive-announced_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-verge-drive-announced-215">[215]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Photos" title="Google Photos">Google Photos</a> for photo storage and sharing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Keep" title="Google Keep">Google Keep</a> for <a href="/wiki/Note-taking" title="Note-taking">note-taking</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Translate" title="Google Translate">Google Translate</a> for language translation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> for video viewing and sharing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_My_Business" title="Google My Business">Google My Business</a> for managing public business information,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Google%2B" title="Google+">Google+</a> (which will be closing down on April 2, 2019),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Google_Allo" title="Google Allo">Allo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Google_Duo" title="Google Duo">Duo</a> for social interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google develops the <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> <a href="/wiki/Mobile_operating_system" title="Mobile operating system">mobile operating system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> as well as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_Wear" title="Android Wear">smartwatch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Android_TV" title="Android TV">television</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Android_Auto" title="Android Auto">car</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">Internet of things</a>-enabled <a href="/wiki/Android_Things" title="Android Things">smart devices</a> variations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>It also develops the <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> web browser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">Chrome OS</a>, an operating system based on Chrome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In January 2010, Google released <a href="/wiki/Nexus_One" title="Nexus One">Nexus One</a>, the first Android phone under its own, "Nexus", brand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> It spawned a number of phones and tablets under the "Nexus" branding<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> until its eventual discontinuation in 2016, replaced by a new brand called, <a href="/wiki/Pixel_(smartphone)" title="Pixel (smartphone)">Pixel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pixel_inside_story_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pixel_inside_story-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Chromebook" title="Chromebook">Chromebook</a> was introduced, described as a "new kind of computer" running <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">Chrome OS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In July 2013, Google introduced the <a href="/wiki/Chromecast" title="Chromecast">Chromecast</a> dongle, that allows users to stream content from their smartphones to televisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In June 2014, Google announced <a href="/wiki/Google_Cardboard" title="Google Cardboard">Google Cardboard</a>, a simple cardboard viewer that lets user place their smartphone in a special front compartment to view <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> (VR) media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In April 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/Recode" title="Recode">Recode</a></i> reported that Google had hired Rick Osterloh, <a href="/wiki/Motorola_Mobility" title="Motorola Mobility">Motorola Mobility</a>'s former President, to head Google's new hardware division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> In October 2016, Osterloh stated that "a lot of the innovation that we want to do now ends up requiring controlling the end-to-end user experience",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pixel_inside_story_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pixel_inside_story-235">[235]</a></sup> and Google announced several hardware platforms:
</p>
<p>In February 2010, Google announced the <a href="/wiki/Google_Fiber" title="Google Fiber">Google Fiber</a> project, with experimental plans to build an ultra-high-speed broadband network for 50,000 to 500,000 customers in one or more American cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> Following Google's corporate restructure to make <a href="/wiki/Alphabet_Inc." title="Alphabet Inc.">Alphabet Inc.</a> its parent company, Google Fiber was moved to Alphabet's Access division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In April 2015, Google announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_Fi" title="Project Fi">Project Fi</a>, a mobile virtual network operator, that combines Wi-Fi and cellular networks from different telecommunication providers in an effort to enable seamless connectivity and fast Internet signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In September 2016, Google began its Google Station initiative, a project for public Wi-Fi at railway stations in India. Caesar Sengupta, VP for Google's next billion users, told <i><a href="/wiki/The_Verge" title="The Verge">The Verge</a></i> that 15,000 people get online for the first time thanks to Google Station and that 3.5 million people use the service every month. The expansion meant that Google was looking for partners around the world to further develop the initiative, which promised "high-quality, secure, easily accessible Wi-Fi".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> By December, Google Station had been deployed at 100 railway stations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> and in February, Google announced its intention to expand beyond railway stations, with a plan to bring citywide Wi-Fi to <a href="/wiki/Pune" title="Pune">Pune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>As of  October 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_S.A." title="Orange S.A.">Orange</a> has teamed up with Google in order to create a <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_communications_cable" title="Transatlantic communications cable">transatlantic</a> undersea cable to share data between the United States and France at faster speeds. Planned to begin operation in 2020, the cable is purported to transfer information at rates “more than 30 terabits per second, per [fibre] pair”. The cable will span approximately 6600 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometers" title="Kilometers">kilometers</a> in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google launched its <a href="/wiki/Google_News" title="Google News">Google News</a> service in 2002, an automated service which summarizes news articles from various websites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> In March 2005, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agence_France_Presse" title="Agence France Presse">Agence France Presse</a> (AFP) sued Google for copyright infringement in federal court in the District of Columbia, a case which Google settled for an undisclosed amount in a pact that included a license of the full text of AFP articles for use on Google News.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In May 2011, Google announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Wallet" title="Google Wallet">Google Wallet</a>, a mobile application for wireless payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2013, Google launched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Shopping_Express" title="Google Shopping Express">Google Shopping Express</a>, a delivery service initially available only in San Francisco and Silicon Valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Google_Alerts" title="Google Alerts">Google Alerts</a> is a content <a href="/wiki/Change_detection_and_notification" title="Change detection and notification">change detection and notification</a> service, offered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Search_engine" title="Search engine">search engine</a> company Google. The service sends emails to the user when it finds new results—such as web pages, newspaper articles, or blogs—that match the user's search term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In July 2015 Google released <a href="/wiki/DeepDream" title="DeepDream">DeepDream</a>, an image recognition software capable of creating psychedelic images using a <a href="/wiki/Convolutional_neural_network" title="Convolutional neural network">convolutional neural network</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-codexample_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-codexample-265">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goingdeeper_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-goingdeeper-266">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><span id="Family_Link"></span>Google introduced its Family Link service in March 2017, letting parents buy <a href="/wiki/Android_Nougat" title="Android Nougat">Android Nougat</a>-based Android devices for kids under 13 years of age and create a Google account through the app, with the parents controlling the apps installed, monitor the time spent using the device, and setting a "Bedtime" feature that remotely locks the device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><span id="AutoDraw"></span>In April 2017, Google launched AutoDraw, a web-based tool using <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> to recognize users' drawings and replace scribbles with related <a href="/wiki/Stock_photography" title="Stock photography">stock images</a> that have been created by professional artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> The tool is built using the same technology as QuickDraw, an experimental game from Google's Creative Lab where users were tasked with drawing objects that algorithms would recognize within 20 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In May 2017, Google added "Family Groups" to several of its services. The feature, which lets users create a group consisting of their family members' individual Google accounts, lets users add their "Family Group" as a collaborator to shared albums in <a href="/wiki/Google_Photos" title="Google Photos">Google Photos</a>, shared notes in <a href="/wiki/Google_Keep" title="Google Keep">Google Keep</a>, and common events in <a href="/wiki/Google_Calendar" title="Google Calendar">Google Calendar</a>. At announcement, the feature is limited to Australia, Brazil, Canada, France, Germany, Ireland, Italy, Japan, Mexico, New Zealand, Russia, Spain, United Kingdom and United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Google_APIs" title="Google APIs">Google APIs</a> are a set of <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">application programming interfaces</a> (APIs) developed by Google which allow communication with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Services" title="Google Services">Google Services</a> and their integration to other services. Examples of these include Search, Gmail, Translate or Google Maps. Third-party apps can use these APIs to take advantage of or extend the functionality of the existing services.
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Google_Developers" title="Google Developers">Google Developers</a> is Google's site for <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">software development</a> tools, APIs, and technical resources. The site contains documentation on using Google developer tools and APIs—including discussion groups and blogs for developers using Google's developer products.
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Google_Labs" title="Google Labs">Google Labs</a> was a page created by Google to demonstrate and test new projects.
</p>
<p>Google owns the top-level domain <b>1e100.net</b> which is used for some servers within Google's network. The name is a reference to the scientific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_notation" title="E notation">E notation</a> representation for 1 googol, <span class="texhtml">1E100 = 1 × 10<sup>100</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In March 2017, Google launched a new website, opensource.google.com, to publish its internal documentation for Google Open Source projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In June 2017, Google launched "We Wear Culture", a searchable archive of 3,000 years of global fashion. The archive, a result of collaboration between Google and over 180 museums, schools, fashion institutes, and other organizations, also offers curated exhibits of specific fashion topics and their impact on society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine's list of the best companies to work for, Google ranked first in 2007, 2008 and 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-best_company_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-best_company-282">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> and fourth in 2009 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> Google was also nominated in 2010 to be the world's most attractive employer to graduating students in the Universum Communications talent attraction index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> Google's corporate philosophy includes principles such as "you can make money without doing evil," "you can be serious without a suit," and "work should be challenging and the challenge should be fun."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><span id="Innovation_Time_Off"></span>
As a motivation technique, Google uses a policy known as Innovation Time Off, where Google engineers are encouraged to spend 20% of their work time on projects that interest them. Some of Google's services, such as Gmail, Google News, <a href="/wiki/Orkut" title="Orkut">Orkut</a>, and AdSense originated from these independent endeavors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> In a talk at Stanford University, <a href="/wiki/Marissa_Mayer" title="Marissa Mayer">Marissa Mayer</a>, Google's Vice-President of Search Products and User Experience until July 2012, showed that half of all new product launches in the second half of 2005 had originated from the Innovation Time Off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On 25 October 2018, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> published the <i><a href="/wiki/Investigative_journalism" title="Investigative journalism">exposé</a></i>, "How Google Protected <a href="/wiki/Andy_Rubin" title="Andy Rubin">Andy Rubin</a>, the ‘Father of Android’". The company subsequently announced that "48 employees have been fired over the last two years" for sexual misconduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup> A week after the article appeared, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_X" title="Google X">Google X</a> (renamed X Development LLC in 2015) executive Rich DeVaul resigned pursuant to  a complaint of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>As of December 2018,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Google has 98,771 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EmployeesAsOf2018Q4_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-EmployeesAsOf2018Q4-4">[4]</a></sup> Google's 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> diversity report states that 31 percent of its workforce are women and 69 percent are men, with the ethnicity of its workforce being predominantly white (56%) and Asian (35%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2017DiversityReport_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-2017DiversityReport-294">[294]</a></sup> Within tech roles, however, 20 percent were women; and 25 percent of leadership roles were held by women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2017DiversityReport_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-2017DiversityReport-294">[294]</a></sup> The report also announced that <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>'s former vice-president, <a href="/wiki/Chief_diversity_officer" title="Chief diversity officer">CDO</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chief_human_resources_officer" title="Chief human resources officer">CHRO</a> Danielle Brown would be joining Google as its new Vice-President of Diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2017DiversityReport_294-2"><a href="#cite_note-2017DiversityReport-294">[294]</a></sup> <span class="cleanup-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">A March 2013 report was presented at EclipseCon2013 which detailed that Google had over 10,000 developers based in more than 40 offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup></span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This information is over five years old and the growth of Google since then suggests that the number might have doubled or tripled since then. Google's Form 10-Q SEC filing for Q1 of 2018 does not appear to break down employee numbers. (May 2018)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<p>Google's employees are hired based on a hierarchical system. Employees are split into six hierarchies based on experience and can range "from entry-level data center workers at level one to managers and experienced engineers at level six."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Following the company's IPO in 2004, founders Sergey Brin and Larry Page and CEO Eric Schmidt requested that their <a href="/wiki/One-dollar_salary" title="One-dollar salary">base salary be cut to $1</a>. Subsequent offers by the company to increase their salaries were turned down, primarily because their main compensation continues to come from owning stock in Google. Before 2004, Schmidt made $250,000 per year, and Page and Brin each received an annual salary of $150,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In March 2008, <a href="/wiki/Sheryl_Sandberg" title="Sheryl Sandberg">Sheryl Sandberg</a>, then vice-president of global online sales and operations, began her position as chief operating officer of Facebook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup>
In 2009, early employee <a href="/wiki/Tim_Armstrong_(executive)" title="Tim Armstrong (executive)">Tim Armstrong</a> left to become CEO of <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">AOL</a>. In July 2012, Google's first female engineer, <a href="/wiki/Marissa_Mayer" title="Marissa Mayer">Marissa Mayer</a>, left Google to become <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>'s CEO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In January 2017, Google employees donated over $2 million to a crisis fund in support of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refugees" title="Refugees">refugees</a>; the company matched the donation with an additional $2 million. Employees then organized a global workplace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walk-out" title="Walk-out">walk-out</a>, aided by the <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">hashtag</a> #GooglersUnite, to protest U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_13769" title="Executive Order 13769">Muslim travel ban</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In late 2017, former <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> executive <a href="/wiki/Diane_Bryant" title="Diane Bryant">Diane Bryant</a> became Chief Operating Officer of <a href="/wiki/Google_Cloud_Platform" title="Google Cloud Platform">Google Cloud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On 1 November 2018, Google employees staged a global walk-out to protest the company's handling of sexual harassment complaints, including the <a href="/wiki/Golden_parachute" title="Golden parachute">golden parachute</a> exit of former executive <a href="/wiki/Andy_Rubin" title="Andy Rubin">Andy Rubin</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> more than 20,000 employees and contractors participated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> CEO <a href="/wiki/Sundar_Pichai" title="Sundar Pichai">Sundar Pichai</a> was reported to be in support of the protests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Mountain_View,_California" title="Mountain View, California">Mountain View</a>, California is referred to as "the <a href="/wiki/Googleplex" title="Googleplex">Googleplex</a>", a play on words on the number <a href="/wiki/Googolplex" title="Googolplex">googolplex</a> and the headquarters itself being a <i>complex</i> of buildings. The lobby is decorated with a piano, <a href="/wiki/Lava_lamp" title="Lava lamp">lava lamps</a>, old server clusters, and a projection of search queries on the wall. The hallways are full of exercise balls and bicycles. Many employees have access to the corporate recreation center. Recreational amenities are scattered throughout the campus and include a workout room with weights and rowing machines, locker rooms, washers and dryers, a massage room, assorted video games, <a href="/wiki/Table_football" title="Table football">table football</a>, a baby grand piano, a billiard table, and ping pong. In addition to the recreation room, there are snack rooms stocked with various foods and drinks, with special emphasis placed on nutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> Free food is available to employees 24/7, with the offerings provided by paid vending machines <a href="/wiki/Pro_rata" title="Pro rata">prorated</a> based on and favoring those of better nutritional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's extensive amenities are not available to all of its workers. Temporary workers such as book scanners do not have access to shuttles, Google cafes, or other perks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2006, Google moved into about 300,000 square feet (27,900 m<sup>2</sup>) of office space in New York City, at <a href="/wiki/111_Eighth_Avenue" title="111 Eighth Avenue">111 Eighth Avenue</a> in Manhattan. The office was designed and built specially for Google, and houses its largest advertising sales team, which has been instrumental in securing large partnerships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> The New York headquarters includes a game room, micro-kitchens, and a video game area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup> In 2010, Google bought the building housing the headquarter, in a deal that valued the property at around $1.9 billion, the biggest for a single building in the United States that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup> In February 2012, Google moved additional employees to the New York City campus, with a total of around 2,750 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's New York City location continued to expand in 2018. In March of that year, Google's parent company Alphabet bought <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Market" title="Chelsea Market">Chelsea Market</a> building for $2.4 billion nearby its current New York HQ. The sale is touted as one of the most expensive real estate transactions for a single building in the history of New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup> The same December, it was announced that a $1 billion, 1,700,000-square-foot (160,000 m<sup>2</sup>) headquarters for Google would be built in Manhattan's <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Square" title="Hudson Square">Hudson Square</a> neighborhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup> Called Google Hudson Square, the new campus is projected to more than double the number of Google employees working in New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>By late 2006, Google established a new headquarters for its AdWords division in <a href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan" title="Ann Arbor, Michigan">Ann Arbor, Michigan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> In November 2006, Google opened offices on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon" title="Carnegie Mellon">Carnegie Mellon</a>'s campus in <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, focusing on shopping-related advertisement coding and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smartphone_applications" title="Smartphone applications">smartphone applications</a> and programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup> Other office locations in the U.S. include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>; <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a>; <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" title="Seattle, Washington">Seattle, Washington</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kirkland,_Washington" title="Kirkland, Washington">Kirkland, Washington</a>; <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Michigan" title="Birmingham, Michigan">Birmingham, Michigan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Reston,_Virginia" title="Reston, Virginia">Reston, Virginia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In October 2006, the company announced plans to install thousands of <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> to provide up to 1.6 mega<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> of electricity, enough to satisfy approximately 30% of the campus' energy needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-solar-326">[326]</a></sup> The system will be the largest <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">solar power system</a> constructed on a U.S. corporate campus and one of the largest on any corporate site in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_326-1"><a href="#cite_note-solar-326">[326]</a></sup> In addition, Google announced in 2009 that it was deploying herds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goats" title="Goats">goats</a> to keep grassland around the Googleplex short, helping to prevent the threat from seasonal bush fires while also reducing the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> of mowing the extensive grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup> The idea of trimming lawns using goats originated from <a href="/wiki/Bob_Widlar" title="Bob Widlar">Bob Widlar</a>, an engineer who worked for <a href="/wiki/National_Semiconductor" title="National Semiconductor">National Semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup> In 2008, Google faced accusations in <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper's Magazine">Harper's Magazine</a></i> of being an "energy glutton". The company was accused of employing its "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_be_evil" title="Don't be evil">Don't be evil</a>" motto and its public <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy-saving" title="Energy-saving">energy-saving</a> campaigns to cover up or make up for the massive amounts of energy its servers require.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Internationally, Google has over 78 offices in more than 50 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup> It also has product research and development operations in cities around the world, namely <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a> (birthplace location of <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">Google Maps</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> (part of <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> development).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In November 2013, Google announced plans for a new <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> headquarter, a notable 1 million square foot office able to accommodate 4,500 employees. Recognized as one of the biggest ever commercial property acquisitions at the time of the deal's announcement in January,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup> Google submitted plans for the new headquarter to the <a href="/wiki/Camden_London_Borough_Council" title="Camden London Borough Council">Camden Council</a> in June 2017. The new building, if approved, will feature a rooftop garden with a running track, giant moving blinds, a swimming pool, and a multi-use games area for sports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In May 2015, Google announced its intention to create its own campus in <a href="/wiki/Hyderabad" title="Hyderabad">Hyderabad</a>, India. The new campus, reported to be the company's largest outside the United States, will accommodate 13,000 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Since  1998,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Google has been designing special, temporary alternate logos to place on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homepage" title="Homepage">homepage</a> intended to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Holiday" title="Holiday">holidays</a>, events, achievements and people. The first Google <a href="/wiki/Doodle" title="Doodle">Doodle</a> was in honor of the <a href="/wiki/Burning_Man" title="Burning Man">Burning Man Festival</a> of 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup> The doodle was designed by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Brin" title="Sergey Brin">Sergey Brin</a> to notify users of their absence in case the servers crashed. Subsequent Google Doodles were designed by an outside contractor, until Larry and Sergey asked then-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intern" title="Intern">intern</a> <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Hwang" title="Dennis Hwang">Dennis Hwang</a> to design a logo for <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day" title="Bastille Day">Bastille Day</a> in 2000. From that point onward, Doodles have been organized and created by a team of employees termed "Doodlers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google has a tradition of creating <a href="/wiki/April_Fools%27_Day" title="April Fools' Day">April Fools' Day</a> jokes. On April 1, 2000, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google%27s_hoaxes#2000" title="Google's hoaxes">Google MentalPlex</a> allegedly featured the use of mental power to search the web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mentalplex_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-mentalplex-342">[342]</a></sup> In 2007, Google announced a free Internet service called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TiSP" title="TiSP">TiSP</a>, or Toilet Internet Service Provider, where one obtained a connection by flushing one end of a <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fiber-optic</a> cable down their toilet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TiSP_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-TiSP-343">[343]</a></sup> Also in 2007, Google's Gmail page displayed an announcement for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gmail_Paper" title="Gmail Paper">Gmail Paper</a>, allowing users to have email messages printed and shipped to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmail_paper_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-gmail_paper-344">[344]</a></sup>
In 2008, Google announced Gmail Custom time where users could change the time that the email was sent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2010, Google changed its company name to Topeka in honor of <a href="/wiki/Topeka,_Kansas" title="Topeka, Kansas">Topeka, Kansas</a>, whose mayor changed the city's name to Google for a short amount of time in an attempt to sway Google's decision in its new <a href="/wiki/Google_Fiber" title="Google Fiber">Google Fiber Project</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Blog:_A_Different_Kind_of_Company_Name_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Blog:_A_Different_Kind_of_Company_Name-346">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup> In 2011, Google announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gmail_Motion" title="Gmail Motion">Gmail Motion</a>, an interactive way of controlling Gmail and the computer with body movements via the user's webcam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[348]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's services contain <a href="/wiki/Easter_egg_(media)" title="Easter egg (media)">easter eggs</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Chef" title="Swedish Chef">Swedish Chef</a>'s "Bork bork bork," <a href="/wiki/Pig_Latin" title="Pig Latin">Pig Latin</a>, "Hacker" or <a href="/wiki/Leet" title="Leet">leetspeak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elmer_Fudd" title="Elmer Fudd">Elmer Fudd</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Talk_Like_a_Pirate_Day" title="International Talk Like a Pirate Day">Pirate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Klingon_language" title="Klingon language">Klingon</a> as language selections for its search engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup> The search engine calculator provides the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Answer_to_the_Ultimate_Question_of_Life,_the_Universe,_and_Everything" title="Answer to the Ultimate Question of Life, the Universe, and Everything">Answer to the Ultimate Question of Life, the Universe, and Everything</a> from <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams">Douglas Adams</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hitchhiker%27s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy" title="The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy">The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[350]</a></sup> When searching the word "recursion", the spell-checker's result for the properly spelled word is exactly the same word, creating a recursive link.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[351]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>When searching for the word "<a href="/wiki/Anagram" title="Anagram">anagram</a>," meaning a rearrangement of letters from one word to form other valid words, Google's suggestion feature displays "Did you mean: nag a ram?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[352]</a></sup> In Google Maps, searching for directions between places separated by large bodies of water, such as Los Angeles and Tokyo, results in instructions to "<a href="/wiki/Kayak" title="Kayak">kayak</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>." During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup_2010" title="FIFA World Cup 2010">FIFA World Cup 2010</a>, search queries including "<a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">World Cup</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>" caused the "Goooo...gle" page indicator at the bottom of every result page to read "Goooo...al!" instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2004, Google formed the not-for-profit philanthropic <a href="/wiki/Google.org" title="Google.org">Google.org</a>, with a start-up fund of $1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philanthropy_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-philanthropy-354">[354]</a></sup> The mission of the organization is to create awareness about <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, global public health, and global poverty. One of its first projects was to develop a viable <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> that can attain 100 miles per gallon. Google hired <a href="/wiki/Larry_Brilliant" title="Larry Brilliant">Larry Brilliant</a> as the program's executive director in 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup> and Megan Smith has since<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> replaced him has director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2008, Google announced its "project 10<sup>100</sup>" which accepted ideas for how to help the community and then allowed Google users to vote on their favorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[357]</a></sup> After two years of silence, during which many wondered what had happened to the program,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[358]</a></sup> Google revealed the winners of the project, giving a total of ten million dollars to various ideas ranging from non-profit organizations that promote education to a website that intends to make all legal documents public and online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[359]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In March 2007, in partnership with the <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Sciences_Research_Institute" title="Mathematical Sciences Research Institute">Mathematical Sciences Research Institute</a> (MSRI), Google hosted the first <a href="/wiki/Julia_Robinson_Mathematics_Festival" title="Julia Robinson Mathematics Festival">Julia Robinson Mathematics Festival</a> at its headquarters in Mountain View.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[360]</a></sup> In 2011, Google donated 1 million euros to <a href="/wiki/International_Mathematical_Olympiad" title="International Mathematical Olympiad">International Mathematical Olympiad</a> to support the next five annual International Mathematical Olympiads (2011–2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[361]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[362]</a></sup> In July 2012, Google launched a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legalize_Love" title="Legalize Love">Legalize Love</a>" campaign in support of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_rights" title="Gay rights">gay rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[363]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google uses various <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax avoidance strategies</a>. Out of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_information_technology_companies" title="List of the largest information technology companies">five largest American technology companies</a>, it pays the lowest taxes to the countries of origin of its revenues. Google between 2007 and 2010 saved $3.1 billion in taxes by shuttling non-U.S. profits through <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>. Such techniques lower its non-U.S. tax rate to 2.3 per cent, while normally the corporate tax rate in for instance the UK is 28 per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup> This has reportedly sparked a French investigation into Google's <a href="/wiki/Transfer_pricing" title="Transfer pricing">transfer pricing</a> practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Following criticism of the amount of corporate taxes that Google paid in the United Kingdom, Chairman Eric Schmidt said, "It's called capitalism. We are proudly capitalistic." During the same December 2012 interview, Schmidt confirmed that the company had no intention of paying more to the UK exchequer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[366]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Matt_Brittin" title="Matt Brittin">Matt Brittin</a> testified to the <a href="/wiki/Public_Accounts_Committee_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public Accounts Committee (United Kingdom)">Public Accounts Committee</a> of the UK House of Commons that his UK sales team made no sales and hence owed no sales taxes to the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[367]</a></sup> In January 2016, Google reached a settlement with the UK to pay £130m in back taxes plus higher taxes in future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[368]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2017, Google channeled $22.7 billion from the Netherlands to Bermuda to reduce its tax bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[369]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Since  2007,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Google has aimed for carbon neutrality in regard to its operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[370]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google disclosed in September 2011 that it "continuously uses enough electricity to power 200,000 homes", almost 260 million watts or about a quarter of the output of a nuclear power plant. Total carbon emissions for 2010 were just under 1.5 million metric tons, mostly due to fossil fuels that provide electricity for the data centers. Google said that 25 percent of its energy was supplied by renewable fuels in 2010. An average search uses only 0.3 watt-hours of electricity, so all global searches are only 12.5 million watts or 5% of the total electricity consumption by Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[371]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2007, Google launched a project centered on developing renewable energy, titled the "Renewable Energy Cheaper than Coal (RE&lt;C)" project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[372]</a></sup> However, the project was canceled in 2014, after engineers Ross Koningstein and David Fork understood, after years of study, that "best-case scenario, which was based on our most optimistic forecasts for renewable energy, would still result in severe climate change", writing that they "came to the conclusion that even if Google and others had led the way toward a wholesale adoption of renewable energy, that switch would not have resulted in significant reductions in carbon dioxide emissions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[373]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In June 2013, <i>The Washington Post</i> reported that Google had donated $50,000 to the <a href="/wiki/Competitive_Enterprise_Institute" title="Competitive Enterprise Institute">Competitive Enterprise Institute</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> that calls human carbon emissions a positive factor in the environment and argues that global warming is not a concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[374]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In July 2013, it was reported that Google had hosted a fundraising event for Oklahoma Senator <a href="/wiki/Jim_Inhofe" title="Jim Inhofe">Jim Inhofe</a>, who has called climate change a "hoax".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup> In 2014 Google cut ties with the <a href="/wiki/American_Legislative_Exchange_Council" title="American Legislative Exchange Council">American Legislative Exchange Council</a> (ALEC) after pressure from the Sierra Club, major unions and Google's own scientists because of ALEC's stance on climate change and opposition to renewable energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LA_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-LA-376">[376]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In November 2017, Google bought 536 megawatts of wind power. The purchase made the firm reach 100% renewable energy. The wind energy comes from two power plants in South Dakota, one in Iowa and one in Oklahoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2013, Google ranked 5th in <a href="/wiki/Lobbying_in_the_United_States" title="Lobbying in the United States">lobbying</a> spending, up from 213th in 2003. In 2012, the company ranked 2nd in campaign donations of technology and Internet sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lobby1_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-lobby1-378">[378]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google has been involved in a number of lawsuits including the <a href="/wiki/High-Tech_Employee_Antitrust_Litigation" title="High-Tech Employee Antitrust Litigation">High-Tech Employee Antitrust Litigation</a> which resulted in Google being one of four companies to pay a $415 million settlement to employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[379]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On June 27, 2017, the company received a record fine of <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">€</a>2.42 billion from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> for "promoting its own shopping comparison service at the top of search results."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup> Commenting on the penalty, <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> magazine said: "The hefty sum – the largest ever doled out by the EU's competition regulators – will sting in the short term, but Google can handle it. Alphabet, Google’s parent company, made a profit of $2.5 billion (€2.2 billion) in the first six weeks of 2017 alone. The real impact of the ruling is that Google must stop using its dominance as a search engine to give itself the edge in another market: online price comparisons." The company disputed the ruling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[381]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On July 18, 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[382]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Competition" title="European Commissioner for Competition">European Commission</a> fined Google €4.34 billion for breaching EU antitrust rules. The abuse of dominant position has been referred to Google's constraint applied on Android device manufacturers and network operators to ensure that traffic on Android devices goes to the Google search engine. On October 9, 2018, Google confirmed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[383]</a></sup> that it had appealed the fine to the <a href="/wiki/General_Court_(European_Union)" title="General Court (European Union)">General Court</a> of the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[384]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On January 21, 2019, French data regulator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CNIL" title="CNIL">CNIL</a> imposed a record €50 million fine on Google for breaching the European Union’s <a href="/wiki/General_Data_Protection_Regulation" title="General Data Protection Regulation">General Data Protection Regulation</a>. The judgment claimed Google had failed to sufficiently inform users of its methods for collecting data to personalize advertising. Google issued a statement saying it was “deeply committed” to transparency and was “studying the decision” before determining its response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[385]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's market dominance has led to prominent media coverage, including <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google" title="Criticism of Google">criticism of the company</a> over issues such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_tax_avoidance" title="Google tax avoidance">aggressive tax avoidance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[386]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google#Page_rank" title="Criticism of Google">search neutrality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Google#Copyright_issues" title="Criticism of Google">copyright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Censorship_by_Google" title="Censorship by Google">censorship</a> of search results and content,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[387]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_privacy" title="Google privacy">privacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[389]</a></sup> Other criticisms include alleged misuse and manipulation of search results, its use of others' <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a>, concerns that its <a href="/wiki/Data_collection" title="Data collection">compilation of data</a> may violate people's <a href="/wiki/Internet_privacy" title="Internet privacy">privacy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Energy_consumption" title="Energy consumption">energy consumption</a> of its servers, as well as concerns over traditional business issues such as <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Restraint_of_trade" title="Restraint of trade">restraint of trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-competitive_practices" title="Anti-competitive practices">anti-competitive practices</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Patent_infringement" title="Patent infringement">patent infringement</a>.
</p>
<p>Former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deputy_Defense_Secretary" title="Deputy Defense Secretary">Deputy Defense Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_O._Work" title="Robert O. Work">Robert O. Work</a> in 2018 criticizes Google and its employees have stepped into a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_Hazard" title="Moral Hazard">Moral Hazard</a></i> for themselves as not continuing Pentagon's <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> project while helping the autocratic communist China's AI technology that could be used against the United States in a conflict. He described Google as hypocritical, given it has opened an AI center in China and “Anything that’s going on in the AI center in China is going to the Chinese government and then will ultimately end up in the hands of the Chinese military." Work said. “I didn’t see any Google employee saying, ‘Hmm, maybe we shouldn’t do that.'” Google's dealings with China is decrying as unpatriotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[390]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[391]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[392]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[393]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google adhered to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Internet censorship in the People's Republic of China">Internet censorship policies of China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[394]</a></sup> enforced by means of filters colloquially known as "The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Firewall_of_China" title="Great Firewall of China">Great Firewall of China</a>". <i>The Intercept</i> reported in August 2018 that Google is developing for the people's Republic of China a censored version of its search engine (known as <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly_(search_engine)" title="Dragonfly (search engine)">Dragonfly</a>) "that will blacklist websites and search terms about human rights, democracy, religion, and peaceful protest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[395]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[396]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> General <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dunford" title="Joseph Dunford">Joseph Dunford</a> also criticizes Google as "it’s inexplicable" that it continue investing in autocratic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">communist China</a>, who uses <a href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_China" title="Internet censorship in China">censorship technology to restrain freedoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_China" title="Mass surveillance in China">crackdown people</a> there and has long history of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> theft which hurts U.S. companies, while simultaneously not renewing further research and development collaborations with <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">the Pentagon</a>. He said “I’m not sure that people at Google will enjoy a world order that is informed by the norms and standards of Russia or China.” He urges Google work directly with the U.S. government instead of making controversial inroads into China. Senator <a href="/wiki/Mark_Warner" title="Mark Warner">Mark Warner</a> (D-VA) criticized Dragonfly evidences China's success at "recruit[ing] Western companies to their information control efforts" while China exports cyber and censorship infrastructure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> regimes like Venezuela, Ethiopia, and Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-397"><a href="#cite_note-397">[397]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-398"><a href="#cite_note-398">[398]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[399]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's <a href="/wiki/Mission_statement" title="Mission statement">mission statement</a>, from the outset, was "to organize the world's information and make it universally accessible and useful",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-400"><a href="#cite_note-400">[400]</a></sup> and its unofficial slogan is "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_be_evil" title="Don't be evil">Don't be evil</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-401"><a href="#cite_note-401">[401]</a></sup> In October 2015, a related motto was adopted in the Alphabet corporate code of conduct by the phrase: "Do the right thing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[402]</a></sup> The original motto was retained in the code of conduct of Google, now a subsidiary of Alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google's commitment to such robust idealism has been increasingly called into doubt due to a number of the firm's actions and behaviors which appear to contradict this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[403]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[404]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Following media reports about <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM</a>, NSA's massive electronic <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance" title="Mass surveillance">surveillance program</a>, in June 2013, several technology companies were identified as participants, including Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-405"><a href="#cite_note-405">[405]</a></sup> According to leaks of said program, Google joined the PRISM program in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-406"><a href="#cite_note-406">[406]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On August 8, 2017, Google fired employee James Damore after he distributed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_memo" title="Google memo">a memo</a> throughout the company which argued that "Google's ideological echo chamber" and bias clouded their thinking about diversity and inclusion, and that it is also biological factors, not discrimination alone, that cause the average woman to be less interested than men in technical positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTFired_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTFired-407">[407]</a></sup> Google CEO Sundar Pichai accused Damore in violating company policy by "advancing harmful gender stereotypes in our workplace", and he was fired on the same day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-408"><a href="#cite_note-408">[408]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-409"><a href="#cite_note-409">[409]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-410"><a href="#cite_note-410">[410]</a></sup> <i>New York Times</i> columnist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Brooks_(political_commentator)" title="David Brooks (political commentator)">David Brooks</a> argued Pichai had mishandled the case, and called for his resignation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrooksNYT_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrooksNYT-411">[411]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-412"><a href="#cite_note-412">[412]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Reportedly, Google's influenced <a href="/wiki/New_America_(organization)" title="New America (organization)">New America</a> think tank to expel their Open Markets research group, after the group has criticized Google monopolistic power and supported the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_vs._Google" title="European Union vs. Google">EU $2.7B fine of Google</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-413"><a href="#cite_note-413">[413]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-414"><a href="#cite_note-414">[414]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>Google has worked with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> on drone software through the 2017 "Project Maven" that could be used to improve the accuracy of <a href="/wiki/Drone_strike" title="Drone strike">drone strikes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-415"><a href="#cite_note-415">[415]</a></sup> Thousands of Google employees, including senior engineers, have signed a letter urging Google CEO <a href="/wiki/Sundar_Pichai" title="Sundar Pichai">Sundar Pichai</a> to end a controversial contract with the Pentagon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-416"><a href="#cite_note-416">[416]</a></sup> In response to the backlash, Google ultimately decided to not renew their DoD contract, set to expire in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-417"><a href="#cite_note-417">[417]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>In 2017, David Elliot and Chris Gillespie argued before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ninth_Circuit_of_the_United_States_Court_of_Appeals" title="Ninth Circuit of the United States Court of Appeals">Ninth Circuit of the United States Court of Appeals</a> that "google" had suffered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genericide" title="Genericide">genericide</a>. The controversy began in 2012 when Gillespie acquired 763 domain names containing the word "google." Google promptly filed a complaint with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Arbitration_Forum" title="National Arbitration Forum">National Arbitration Forum</a> (NAF). Elliot then filed a petition for canceling the Google trademark. Ultimately, the court ruled in favor of Google because Elliot failed to show a preponderance of evidence showing the genericide of "google."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-418"><a href="#cite_note-418">[418]</a></sup>
</p>
<p>On 10 December 2018, a New Zealand court ordered that the name of a man accused of murdering British traveller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grace_Millane" title="Grace Millane">Grace Millane</a> be withheld from the public. The next morning, Google named the man in an email it sent people who had subscribed to "what's trending in New Zealand".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-419"><a href="#cite_note-419">[419]</a></sup> Lawyers warned that this could compromise the trial, and Justice Minister Andrew Little said that Google was in contempt of court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-420"><a href="#cite_note-420">[420]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-421"><a href="#cite_note-421">[421]</a></sup> Google said that it had been unaware of the court order, and that the email had been created by algorithms.
</p>
<p>On 21 January 2019, Google was fined €50 million (US$57 million) for violating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDPR" title="GDPR">GDPR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-422"><a href="#cite_note-422">[422]</a></sup>  It is the biggest fine issued by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CNIL" title="CNIL">CNIL</a> for violation of the regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-423"><a href="#cite_note-423">[423]</a></sup>
</p>

Google LLC[5]is an American multinational technology company that specializes in Internet-related services and products, which includeonline advertising technologies,search engine,cloud computing, software, and hardware. It is considered one of theBig Fourtechnology companies, alongsideAmazon,AppleandFacebook.[6][7]
Google was founded in 1998 byLarry PageandSergey Brinwhile they were Ph.D. students atStanford UniversityinCalifornia. Together they own about 14 percent of its shares and control 56 percent of the stockholder voting power throughsupervotingstock. They incorporated Google as a privately held company on September 4, 1998. An initial public offering (IPO) took place on August 19, 2004, and Google moved to its headquarters in Mountain View, California, nicknamed theGoogleplex. In August 2015, Google announced plans to reorganize its various interests as a conglomerate calledAlphabet Inc.Google is Alphabet's leading subsidiary and will continue to be the umbrella company for Alphabet's Internet interests.Sundar Pichaiwas appointed CEO of Google, replacing Larry Page who became the CEO of Alphabet.
The company's rapid growth since incorporation has triggered a chain of products, acquisitions, and partnerships beyond Google's core search engine (Google Search). It offers services designed for work and productivity (Google Docs,Google Sheets, andGoogle Slides), email (Gmail/Inbox), scheduling and time management (Google Calendar), cloud storage (Google Drive), social networking (Google+), instant messaging and video chat (Google Allo,Duo,Hangouts), language translation (Google Translate), mapping and navigation (Google Maps,Waze,Google Earth,Street View), video sharing (YouTube), note-taking (Google Keep), and photo organizing and editing (Google Photos). The company leads the development of theAndroidmobile operating system, theGoogle Chromeweb browser, andChrome OS, a lightweight operating system based on the Chrome browser. Google has moved increasingly into hardware; from 2010 to 2015, it partnered with major electronics manufacturers in the production of itsNexusdevices, and it released multiple hardware products in October 2016, including theGoogle Pixelsmartphone,Google Homesmart speaker,Google Wifimesh wireless router, andGoogle Daydreamvirtual reality headset. Google has also experimented with becoming an Internet carrier (Google Fiber,Project Fi, andGoogle Station).[8]
Google.com is the most visited website in the world.[9]Several other Google services also figure in the top 100 most visited websites, includingYouTubeandBlogger. Google is the most valuable brand in the world as of  2017,[update][10]but has received significantcriticisminvolving issues such asprivacy concerns, tax avoidance, antitrust,censorship, and search neutrality. Google'smission statementis "to organize the world's information and make it universally accessible and useful", and its unofficial slogan was "Don't be evil" until the phrase was removed from the company's code of conduct around May 2018.[11][12]
Google began in January 1996 as a research project byLarry PageandSergey Brinwhen they were both PhD students atStanford UniversityinStanford, California.[14]
While conventional search engines ranked results by counting how many times the search terms appeared on the page, the two theorized about a better system that analyzed the relationships among websites.[15]They called this new technologyPageRank; it determined a website'srelevanceby the number of pages, and the importance of those pages that linked back to the original site.[16][17]
Page and Brin originally nicknamed their new search engine "BackRub", because the system checked backlinks to estimate the importance of a site.[18][19][20]Eventually, they changed the name to Google; the name of the search engine originated from a misspelling of the word "googol",[21][22]the number 1 followed by 100 zeros, which was picked to signify that the search engine was intended to provide large quantities of information.[23]Originally, Google ran under Stanford University's website, with the domainsgoogle.stanford.edu[24]andz.stanford.edu.[25]
The domain name for Google was registered on September 15, 1997,[26]and the company was incorporated on September 4, 1998. It was based in the garage of a friend (Susan Wojcicki[14]) inMenlo Park, California.Craig Silverstein, a fellow PhD student at Stanford, was hired as the first employee.[14][27][28]
Google was initially funded by an August 1998 contribution of $100,000 fromAndy Bechtolsheim, co-founder ofSun Microsystems; the money was given before Google was incorporated.[30]Google received money from three otherangel investorsin 1998:Amazon.comfounderJeff Bezos, Stanford University computer science professorDavid Cheriton, and entrepreneurRam Shriram.[31]
After some additional, small investments through the end of 1998 to early 1999,[31]a new $25 million round of funding was announced on June 7, 1999,[32]with major investors including theventure capitalfirmsKleiner PerkinsandSequoia Capital.[30]
Early in 1999, Brin and Page decided they wanted to sell Google toExcite. They went to Excite CEO George Bell and offered to sell it to him for $1 million. He rejected the offer.Vinod Khosla, one of Excite's venture capitalists, talked the duo down to $750,000, but Bell still rejected it.[33]
Google'sinitial public offering(IPO) took place five years later, on August 19, 2004. At that time Larry Page, Sergey Brin, andEric Schmidtagreed to work together at Google for 20 years, until the year 2024.[34]
At IPO, the company offered 19,605,052 shares at a price of $85 per share.[35][36]Shares were sold in an online auction format using a system built byMorgan StanleyandCredit Suisse, underwriters for the deal.[37][38]The sale of $1.67 bn (billion) gave Google amarket capitalizationof more than $23bn.[39]By January 2014, its market capitalization had grown to $397bn.[40]The vast majority of the 271 million shares remained under the control of Google, and many Google employees became instant paper millionaires.Yahoo!, a competitor of Google, also benefitted because it owned 8.4 million shares of Google before the IPO took place.[41]
There were concerns that Google's IPO would lead to changes in company culture.
